	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">David Lloyd George</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Lloyd George" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Lloyd George (disambiguation)">Lloyd George (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Former Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This <a href="/wiki/Double-barrelled_name#British_tradition" title="Double-barrelled name">British surname is barrelled</a>, being made up of multiple names. It should be written as <i>Lloyd George</i>, not <i>George</i>.</div>

<p><b>David Lloyd George, 1st Earl Lloyd-George of Dwyfor</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:100%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a><span style="font-size:14px;">&#44;</span>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a></span> (17 January 1863 – 26 March 1945) was a British statesman and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> politician.  He is best known for his roles in mobilising British resources in the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, and then for leading Britain to victory over Germany. 
</p><p>As <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> (1908–1915) during <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>'s tenure as Prime Minister, Lloyd George was a key figure in the introduction of many reforms which laid the foundations of the modern <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">welfare state</a>. His most important role came as the highly energetic Prime Minister of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_coalition_government_(1916%E2%80%931922)" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom coalition government (1916–1922)">Wartime Coalition</a> Government (1916–22), during and immediately after the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. He was a major player at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> of 1919 that reordered Europe after the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>. Although he remained Prime Minister after the 1918 general election, the Conservatives were the largest party in the coalition, with the Liberals split between those loyal to Lloyd George, and those still supporting Asquith. He became the leader of the Liberal Party in the late 1920s, but it grew even smaller and more divided. By the 1930s he was a marginalised and widely mistrusted figure. He gave weak support to the war effort during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> amidst fears that he was favourable toward Germany. 
</p><p>He was voted the <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">third-greatest British prime minister</a> of the 20th century in a poll of 139 academics organised by the market-research company <a href="/wiki/MORI" class="mw-redirect" title="MORI">MORI</a>, and was named among the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a> in a UK-wide vote in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Upbringing_and_early_life">Upbringing and early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Upbringing and early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lloyd George was born on 17 January 1863 in <a href="/wiki/Chorlton-on-Medlock" title="Chorlton-on-Medlock">Chorlton-on-Medlock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> parents, and was brought up as a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>-speaker. He is so far the only British Prime Minister to have been Welsh<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> and to have spoken English as a second language.<sup id="cite_ref-Toby_Harnden_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toby_Harnden-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His father, William George, had been a teacher in both London and Liverpool. He also taught in the Hope Street Sunday Schools, which were administered by the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_Unitarian_and_Free_Christian_Churches" title="General Assembly of Unitarian and Free Christian Churches">Unitarians</a>, where he met Unitarian minister <a href="/wiki/James_Martineau" title="James Martineau">Dr James Martineau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In March of the same year, on account of his failing health, William George returned with his family to his native <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a>. He took up farming but died in June 1864 of <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, aged 44. His widow, Elizabeth George (1828–96), sold the farm and moved with her children to her native <a href="/wiki/Llanystumdwy" title="Llanystumdwy">Llanystumdwy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Caernarfonshire" title="Caernarfonshire">Caernarfonshire</a>, where she lived in a cottage known as Highgate with her brother Richard Lloyd (1834–1917), who was a shoemaker, a <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">minister</a> (in the <a href="/wiki/Baptist_Union_of_Scotland" title="Baptist Union of Scotland">Scottish Baptists</a> and then the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_Movement#Great_Britain" title="Restoration Movement">Church of Christ</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and a strong Liberal. Lloyd George was educated at the local <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> school Llanystumdwy <a href="/wiki/National_school_(England_and_Wales)" title="National school (England and Wales)">National School</a> and later under tutors. Lloyd George's uncle was a towering influence on him, encouraging him to take up a career in law and enter politics; his uncle remained influential up until his death at age 83 in February 1917, by which time his nephew had become Prime Minister. He added his uncle's surname to become "Lloyd George". His surname is usually given as "Lloyd George" and sometimes as "George". The influence of his childhood showed through in his entire career, as he attempted to aid the common man at the expense of what he liked to call "the Dukes" (that is, the aristocracy); however, biographer <a href="/wiki/John_Grigg_(writer)" title="John Grigg (writer)">John Grigg</a> argued that Lloyd George's childhood was nowhere near as poverty-stricken as he liked to suggest.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Brought up a devout evangelical, as a young man he suddenly lost his religious faith. Biographer Don Cregier says he became "a Deist and perhaps an agnostic, though he remained a chapel-goer and connoisseur of good preaching all his life". He kept quiet about this, and was, according to <a href="/wiki/Frank_Owen_(politician)" title="Frank Owen (politician)">Frank Owen</a>, for 25 years "one of the foremost fighting leaders of a fanatical Welsh <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">Nonconformity</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Cregier_1976_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cregier_1976-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was also during this period of his life that Lloyd George first became interested in the issue of land ownership. As a young man he read books by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Spence" title="Thomas Spence">Thomas Spence</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>, as well as pamphlets written by <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney Webb</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> on the issue of land ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> By the age of twenty-one, he had already read and taken notes on <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Progress_and_Poverty" title="Progress and Poverty">Progress and Poverty</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This strongly influenced Lloyd George's politics later in life; the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a> drew heavily on <a href="/wiki/Georgism" title="Georgism">Georgist</a> tax reform ideas.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg/170px-David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg/255px-David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="270" data-file-height="439" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_NLW3362532_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lloyd George circa 1890</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Articled_clerk" title="Articled clerk">Articled</a> to a firm of <a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Porthmadog" title="Porthmadog">Porthmadog</a>, Lloyd George was admitted in 1884 after taking Honours in his final law examination and set up his own practice in the back parlour of his uncle's house in 1885. The practice flourished, and he established branch offices in surrounding towns, taking his brother William into partnership in 1887. Although many Prime Ministers have been <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barristers</a>, Lloyd George is to date the only <a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitor</a> to have held that office.<sup id="cite_ref-Bourns2016_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bourns2016-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By then he was politically active, having campaigned for the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1885_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1885 United Kingdom general election">1885 election</a>, attracted by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>'s "unauthorised programme" of reforms. The election resulted firstly in a stalemate with neither the Liberals nor the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> having a majority, the balance of power being held by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>'s proposal to bring about Irish <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule">Home Rule</a> split the party, with Chamberlain eventually leading the breakaway <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist_Party" title="Liberal Unionist Party">Liberal Unionists</a>. Uncertain of which wing to follow, Lloyd George carried a pro-Chamberlain resolution at the local Liberal Club and travelled to <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a> to attend the first meeting of Chamberlain's National Radical Union, but he had his dates wrong and arrived a week too early. In 1907 he was to say that he thought Chamberlain's plan for a federal solution correct in 1886 and still thought so, that he preferred the unauthorised programme to the <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a>-like platform of the official Liberal Party, and that, had Chamberlain proposed solutions to Welsh grievances such as <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disestablishment" class="mw-redirect" title="Disestablishment">disestablishment</a>, he, together with most Welsh Liberals, would have followed Chamberlain.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1988_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1988-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2019)">failed verification</span></a>&#32;(<a href="/wiki/Talk:David_Lloyd_George#&quot;Lloyd_George_and_Wales&quot;_failed_verification" title="Talk:David Lloyd George">See discussion.</a>)</i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>He married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Lloyd_George" title="Margaret Lloyd George">Margaret Owen</a>, the daughter of a well-to-do local farming family, on 24 January 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Also in that year, he and other young Welsh Liberals founded a monthly paper <i>Udgorn Rhyddid</i> (Bugle of Freedom). They also won the Llanfrothen burial case, which established the right of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconformists</a> to be buried according to their own denominational rites in parish burial grounds, a right given by the <a href="/wiki/Burial_Laws_Amendment_Act_1880" title="Burial Laws Amendment Act 1880">Burial Laws Amendment Act 1880</a> but which had up to then been ignored by the Anglican clergy. On Lloyd George's advice, a Baptist burial party broke open a gate to a cemetery which had been locked against them by the vicar. The vicar sued them for trespass and the local judge misrecorded the jury's verdict and found in his favour, awakening suspicions of bias by the local Tory landowning class. Lloyd George's clients won on <a href="/wiki/Appeal" title="Appeal">appeal</a> to the Divisional Court of Queen's Bench in London, where <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chief_Justice_of_England_and_Wales" title="Lord Chief Justice of England and Wales">Lord Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Coleridge,_1st_Baron_Coleridge" title="John Coleridge, 1st Baron Coleridge">Coleridge</a> found in their favour. It was this case, which was hailed as a great victory throughout Wales, and his writings in <i>Udgorn Rhyddid</i> that led to his adoption as the Liberal candidate for <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Caernarfon (UK Parliament constituency)">Carnarvon Boroughs</a> on 27 December 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1988_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1988-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2019)">failed verification</span></a>&#32;(<a href="/wiki/Talk:David_Lloyd_George#&quot;Lloyd_George_and_Wales&quot;_failed_verification" title="Talk:David Lloyd George">See discussion.</a>)</i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1889 he became an <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">Alderman</a> on <a href="/wiki/Carnarvonshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnarvonshire">Carnarvonshire</a> <a href="/wiki/County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="County Council">County Council</a> which had been created by the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1888" title="Local Government Act 1888">Local Government Act 1888</a>. At that time he appeared to be trying to create a separate Welsh <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">national party</a> modelled on <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Parnell</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> and worked towards a union of the North and South Wales Liberal Federations. For the same county Lloyd George would also become a <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_Peace" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice of the Peace">JP</a> (1910)<sup id="cite_ref-kellys_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kellys-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and chairman of <a href="/wiki/Quarter_Sessions" class="mw-redirect" title="Quarter Sessions">Quarter Sessions</a> (1929–38),<sup id="cite_ref-bpeerage_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bpeerage-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Lieutenant">Deputy Lieutenant</a> in 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-kellys_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kellys-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament">Member of Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Member of Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lloyd George was returned as Liberal MP for <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Caernarfon (UK Parliament constituency)">Carnarvon Boroughs</a> – by a margin of 18 votes – in a <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a> on 10 April 1890, following the death of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> member <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Swetenham" title="Edmund Swetenham">Edmund Swetenham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> He sat with an informal grouping of Welsh Liberal members who had a programme of disestablishing and disendowing the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> in Wales, <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement_in_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperance movement in Wales">temperance</a> reform, and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_home_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh home rule">Welsh home rule</a>. He would remain an MP for the same constituency until 1945, 55 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As backbench members of the House of Commons were not paid at that time, he supported himself and his growing family by continuing to practise as a <a href="/wiki/Solicitor" title="Solicitor">solicitor</a>, opening an office in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> under the name of 'Lloyd George and Co.' and continuing in partnership with William George in <a href="/wiki/Criccieth" title="Criccieth">Criccieth</a>. In 1897 he merged his growing London practice with that of Arthur Rhys Roberts (who was to become <a href="/wiki/Official_Solicitor" title="Official Solicitor">Official Solicitor</a>) under the name of 'Lloyd George, Roberts and Co.'.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He served as  the legal adviser of <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Herzl" title="Theodor Herzl">Theodor Herzl</a> in his negotiations with the British government regarding the  <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Scheme" title="Uganda Scheme">Uganda Scheme</a>, proposed as an alternative homeland for the Jews due to Turkish refusal to grant a charter for Jewish settlement in Palestine.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>He was soon speaking on Liberal issues (particularly temperance – the "<a href="/wiki/Local_option" title="Local option">local option</a>" – and national as opposed to denominational education) throughout England as well as Wales. During the next decade Lloyd George campaigned in Parliament largely on Welsh issues and in particular for disestablishment and disendowment of the Church of England.  He wrote extensively for Liberal papers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i>. When <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone</a> retired in 1894 after the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Home Rule Bill">second Home Rule Bill</a>, the Welsh Liberal members chose him to serve on a deputation to <a href="/wiki/William_Vernon_Harcourt_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Vernon Harcourt (politician)">William Harcourt</a> to press for specific assurances on Welsh issues; when those were not provided, they resolved to take independent action if the government did not bring a bill for disestablishment. When that was not forthcoming, he and three other Welsh Liberals (<a href="/wiki/D._A._Thomas" title="D. A. Thomas">D. A. Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Lewis" title="Herbert Lewis">Herbert Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Edwards_(British_politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Edwards (British politician)">Frank Edwards</a>) refused the <a href="/wiki/Whip_(politics)" title="Whip (politics)">whip</a> on 14 April 1894, but accepted <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a>'s assurance and rejoined the official Liberals on 29 May. Thereafter he devoted much time to setting up branches of <a href="/wiki/Cymru_Fydd" title="Cymru Fydd">Cymru Fydd</a> (Young Wales), which, he said, would in time become a force like the <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish National Party">Irish National Party</a>. He abandoned this idea after being criticised in Welsh newspapers for bringing about the defeat of the Liberal Party in the <a href="/wiki/1895_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1895 United Kingdom general election">1895 election</a> and at a meeting in <a href="/wiki/Newport,_Wales" title="Newport, Wales">Newport</a> on 16 January 1896 of the South Wales Liberal Federation, led by D. A. Thomas, he was shouted down.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George also supported the idea of <a href="/wiki/Pan-Celticism" title="Pan-Celticism">Pan-Celtic unity</a> and gave a speech at the 1904 <a href="/wiki/Pan-Celtic_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Pan-Celtic Congress">Pan-Celtic Congress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon" title="Caernarfon">Caernarfon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposes_Boer_War">Opposes Boer War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Opposes Boer War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lloyd George had been impressed by his journey to Canada in 1899. Although sometimes wrongly supposed – both at the time and subsequently – to be a <a href="/wiki/Little_Englander" title="Little Englander">Little Englander</a>, he was not an opponent of the British Empire <i>per se</i>, but in a speech at <a href="/wiki/Birkenhead" title="Birkenhead">Birkenhead</a> (21 November 1901) he stressed that it needed to be based on freedom, including for <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, not "racial arrogance".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, he gained national fame by displaying vehement opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Rosebery's lead he based his attack firstly on what were supposed to be Britain's war aims – remedying the grievances of the <a href="/wiki/Uitlander" title="Uitlander">Uitlanders</a> and in particular the claim that they were wrongly denied the right to vote, saying "I do not believe the war has any connection with the franchise. It is a question of 45% dividends" and that England (which did not then have universal male suffrage) was more in need of franchise reform than the Boer republics. A second attack came on the cost of the war, which, he argued, prevented overdue social reform in England, such as old age pensions and workmen's cottages. As the fighting continued his attacks moved to its conduct by the generals, who, he said (basing his words on reports by <a href="/wiki/William_Burdett-Coutts" title="William Burdett-Coutts">William Burdett-Coutts</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>), were not providing for the sick or wounded soldiers and were starving Boer women and children in concentration camps. But his major thrusts were reserved for the Chamberlains, accusing them of <a href="/wiki/War_profiteering" title="War profiteering">war profiteering</a> through the family company <a href="/wiki/Kynoch" title="Kynoch">Kynoch</a> Ltd, of which Chamberlain's brother was Chairman. The firm had won tenders to the <a href="/wiki/War_Office" title="War Office">War Office</a> though its prices were higher than some of its competitors. After speaking at a meeting in Birmingham Lloyd George had to be smuggled out disguised as a policeman, as his life was in danger from the mob. At this time the Liberal Party was badly split as <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Haldane,_1st_Viscount_Haldane" title="Richard Haldane, 1st Viscount Haldane">R. B. Haldane</a> and others were supporters of the war and formed the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Liberal_Imperial_League&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Liberal Imperial League (page does not exist)">Liberal Imperial League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg/170px-David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg/255px-David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg/340px-David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1902.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George in 1902</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposes_Education_Act_of_1902">Opposes Education Act of 1902</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Opposes Education Act of 1902">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lloyd George was the main spokesman for the Nonconformists, and they made a major issue out of the government's <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act 1902</a>. It provided funding for Church of England schools, paid out of local taxation. The bill passed but opposition to it helped reunite the Liberals. His successful amendment that county councils need only fund those schools where the buildings were in good repair served to make the Act a dead letter in Wales, where the counties were able to show that most Church of England schools were in poor repair. Having already gained national recognition for his anti-Boer War campaigns, his leadership of the attacks on the Education Act gave him a strong parliamentary reputation and marked him as a likely future cabinet member.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The Act served to reunify the Liberals after their divisions over the Boer War, and to increase Nonconformist influence in the party, which then included educational reform as policy in the <a href="/wiki/1906_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1906 United Kingdom general election">1906 election</a>, which resulted in a Liberal landslide.<sup id="cite_ref-Daglish1994_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daglish1994-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="President_of_the_Board_of_Trade_.281905.E2.80.931908.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="President_of_the_Board_of_Trade_(1905–1908)">President of the Board of Trade (1905–1908)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: President of the Board of Trade (1905–1908)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/170px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/255px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg/340px-ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1808" data-file-height="2284" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChurchillGeorge0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in 1907</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1902#The_failed_Education_Bill_of_1906" title="Education Act 1902">Education Act 1902 §&#160;The failed Education Bill of 1906</a></div>
<p>In 1905, Lloyd George entered the new Liberal Cabinet of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first priority on taking office was repeal of the 1902 Education Act. Lloyd George took the lead along with <a href="/wiki/Augustine_Birrell" title="Augustine Birrell">Augustine Birrell</a>, President of the Board of Education. Lloyd George appears to have been the dominant figure on the committee drawing up the bill in its later stages, and insisted that the bill create a separate education committee for Wales. Birrell complained privately that the bill, introduced in the Commons on 9 April 1906, owed more to Lloyd George and that he himself had had little say in its contents.<sup id="cite_ref-Crosby2014p74-77_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby2014p74-77-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The bill passed the House of Commons greatly amended, but was completely mangled by the House of Lords.<sup id="cite_ref-Daglish1994_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daglish1994-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> For the rest of the year, Lloyd George made numerous public speeches attacking the House of Lords for mutilating the bill with wrecking amendments, in defiance of the Liberals’ electoral mandate to reform the 1902 Act. Lloyd George was rebuked by King Edward VII for these speeches: the Prime Minister defended him to the Kings’s secretary <a href="/wiki/Francis_Knollys,_1st_Viscount_Knollys" title="Francis Knollys, 1st Viscount Knollys">Francis Knollys</a>, stating that his behaviour in Parliament was more constructive but that in speeches to the public “the combative spirit seems to get the better of him”.<sup id="cite_ref-Crosby2014p74-77_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby2014p74-77-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> No compromise was possible and the bill was abandoned, allowing the 1902 Act to continue in effect.<sup id="cite_ref-Daglish1994_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daglish1994-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> As a result of Lloyd George’s lobbying, a separate department for Wales<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> was created within the Board of Education.<sup id="cite_ref-Crosby2014p74-77_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crosby2014p74-77-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nonconformists were bitterly upset by the failure of the Liberal Party to carry through on its most important promise to them, and over time their support for the Liberal Party slowly fell away.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards1972_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards1972-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the Board of Trade Lloyd George introduced legislation on many topics, from <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Shipping_Act_1906" title="Merchant Shipping Act 1906">merchant shipping</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Act_1908" title="Port of London Act 1908">Port of London</a> to <a href="/wiki/Companies_Act_1907" title="Companies Act 1907">companies</a> and railway regulation. His main achievement was in stopping a proposed national strike of the railway unions by brokering an agreement between the unions and the railway companies. While almost all the companies refused to recognise the unions, Lloyd George persuaded the companies to recognise elected representatives of the workers who sat with the company representatives on conciliation boards—one for each company. If those boards failed to agree then there was a central board.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_.281908.E2.80.931915.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_(1908–1915)">Chancellor of the Exchequer (1908–1915)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chancellor of the Exchequer (1908–1915)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On Campbell-Bannerman's death he succeeded Asquith, who had become Prime Minister, as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> from 1908 to 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> While he continued some work from the Board of Trade—for example, legislation to establish the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a> and to pursue traditional Liberal programmes such as licensing law reforms—his first major trial in this role was over the 1908–1909 Naval Estimates. The Liberal manifesto at the <a href="/wiki/1906_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1906 United Kingdom general election">1906 general election</a> included a commitment to reduce military expenditure. Lloyd George strongly supported this, writing to <a href="/wiki/Reginald_McKenna" title="Reginald McKenna">Reginald McKenna</a>, First Lord of the Admiralty, "the emphatic pledges given by all of us at the last general election to reduce the gigantic expenditure on armaments built up by the recklessness of our predecessors."  He then proposed the programme be reduced from six to four <a href="/wiki/Dreadnoughts" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreadnoughts">dreadnoughts</a>. This was adopted by the government, but there was a public storm when the Conservatives, with covert support from the First Sea Lord, Admiral <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Jackie Fisher</a>, campaigned for more with the slogan "We want eight and we won't wait". This resulted in Lloyd George's defeat in Cabinet and the adoption of estimates including provision for eight dreadnoughts.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg/170px-David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg/255px-David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg/340px-David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg 2x" data-file-width="543" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1911.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Chancellor Lloyd George by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Williams_(Welsh_artist)" title="Christopher Williams (Welsh artist)">Christopher Williams</a> (1911)</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="People.27s_Budget.2C_1909"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="People's_Budget,_1909">People's Budget, 1909</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: People&#039;s Budget, 1909">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a></div>
<p>In 1909 Lloyd George introduced his <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a>, imposing a 20% tax on the unearned increase in value of <a href="/wiki/Land_value_tax" title="Land value tax">land</a>, payable at death of the owner or sale of the land, and ½ d. on undeveloped land and minerals, increased death duties, a rise in income tax, and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Supertax" class="mw-redirect" title="Supertax">Supertax</a> on income over £3,000.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> There were taxes also on luxuries, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">alcohol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, so that money could be made available for the new welfare programmes as well as new battleships. The nation's landowners (well represented in the House of Lords) were intensely angry at the new taxes, mostly at the proposed very high tax on <a href="/wiki/Land_value_tax" title="Land value tax">land values</a>, but also because the instrumental redistribution of wealth could be used to detract from an argument for protective tariffs.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The immediate consequences included the end of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_League_(United_Kingdom)" title="Liberal League (United Kingdom)">Liberal League</a>, and Rosebery breaking friendship with the Liberal Party, which in itself was for Lloyd George a triumph. He had won the case of social reform without losing the debate on Free Trade.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a> denounced the budget as "vindictive, inequitable, based on no principles, and injurious to the productive capacity of the country."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Roy Jenkins</a> described it as the most reverberating since Gladstone's in 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the House of Commons Lloyd George gave a brilliant account of the budget, which was attacked by the Conservatives. On the stump, notably at his Limehouse speech in 1909, he denounced the Conservatives and the wealthy classes with all his very considerable oratorical power. The budget was defeated by the Conservative majority in the House of Lords. The elections of 1910 narrowly upheld the Liberal government. The 1909 budget was passed on 28 April 1910 by the Lords, and received the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> on the 29th.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a> curtailed the veto power of the House of Lords.
</p><p>Although old-age pensions had already been introduced by Asquith as Chancellor, Lloyd George was largely responsible for the introduction of state financial support for the sick and infirm (known colloquially as "going on the Lloyd George" for decades afterwards)—legislation referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Reforms">Liberal Reforms</a>. Lloyd George also succeeded in putting through Parliament his <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance_Act_1911" title="National Insurance Act 1911">National Insurance Act 1911</a>, making provision for sickness and invalidism, and a system of unemployment insurance. He was helped in his endeavours by forty or so backbenchers who regularly pushed for new social measures, often voted with Labour MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> These social reforms in Britain were the beginnings of a <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> and fulfilled the aim of dampening down the demands of the growing working class for rather more radical solutions to their impoverishment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Under his leadership after 1909 the Liberals extended minimum wages to farm workers.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg/220px-David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg/330px-David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg/440px-David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="624" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_c1911.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George circa 1911</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Mansion_House_Speech.2C_1911"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mansion_House_Speech,_1911">Mansion House Speech, 1911</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mansion House Speech, 1911">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lloyd George was considered an opponent of war until the <a href="/wiki/Second_Moroccan_Crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Moroccan Crisis">Agadir Crisis</a> of 1911, during which he gave a stirring and patriotic speech at Mansion House on 21 July 1911. Grey was aghast and felt that the Chancellor was more qualified to be Foreign Secretary than he was; German opinion recognised that Britain would resist further German aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Haldane and Lloyd George were among the minority in the Cabinet who were pro-German, on grounds of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">a shared religion</a>, philosophy, artistic culture and scientific enquiry. Germany blamed Lloyd George for doing "untold harm both with regard to German public opinion and the negotiations...namely, to the despatch of <a href="/wiki/SMS_Panther" title="SMS Panther">the German warship</a> to Agadir", and citing <a href="/wiki/Count_Metternich" class="mw-redirect" title="Count Metternich">Count Metternich</a> "...Mr Lloyd George's speech came upon us like a thunderbolt"<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marconi_scandal_1913">Marconi scandal 1913</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Marconi scandal 1913">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1913 Lloyd George, along with <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Isaacs,_1st_Marquess_of_Reading" title="Rufus Isaacs, 1st Marquess of Reading">Rufus Isaacs</a>, the Attorney General, was involved in the <a href="/wiki/Marconi_scandal" title="Marconi scandal">Marconi scandal</a>. Accused of speculating in Marconi shares on the inside information that they were about to be awarded a key government contract (which would have caused them to increase in value), he told the House of Commons that he had not speculated in the shares of "that company". He had in fact bought shares in the American Marconi Company.<sup id="cite_ref-Pugh1988p59-61_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh1988p59-61-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Welsh_Church_Act_1914">Welsh Church Act 1914</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Welsh Church Act 1914">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> no longer had majority adherence in most parts of Wales in preference to Wales-led <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>. Lloyd George had long called for disestablishment and was instrumental in introducing the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Church_Act_1914" title="Welsh Church Act 1914">Welsh Church Act 1914</a> which disestablished the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> in Wales (though, upon the outbreak of war, the actual <a href="/wiki/Coming_into_force" title="Coming into force">coming into force</a> of the Act was postponed by the <a href="/wiki/Suspensory_Act_1914" title="Suspensory Act 1914">Suspensory Act 1914</a> until 1920), removing the opportunity of the six Welsh Bishops in the new <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> to sit in the House of Lords and removing (disendowing) certain pre-1662 property rights.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">First World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: First World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lloyd George was as surprised as almost everyone else by the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>. On 23 July 1914, almost a month after the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria</a> and on the eve of the Austro-Hungarian ultimatum to Serbia, he made a speech advocating "economy" in the House of Commons, saying that Britain's relations with Germany were better than for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> On 27 July he told <a href="/wiki/C._P._Scott" title="C. P. Scott">C. P. Scott</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i> that Britain would keep out of the impending war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss1985156_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss1985156-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> With the Cabinet divided, and most ministers reluctant for Britain to get involved, he struck Asquith as "statesmanlike" at the Cabinet meeting on 1 August, favouring keeping Britain's options open. The next day he seemed likely to resign if Britain intervened, but he held back at Cabinet on Monday 3 August, moved by news that Belgium would resist Germany's demand for passage for her army across her soil. He was seen as a key figure whose stance helped to persuade almost the entire Cabinet to support British intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss1985157-9_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss1985157-9-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He was able to give the more pacifist members of the cabinet and the Liberal Party a principle - the rights of small nations - which meant they could support the war and maintain united political and popular support.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George remained in office as Chancellor of the Exchequer for the first year of the Great War. The budget of 17 November 1914 had to allow for lower taxation receipts because of the reduction in world trade. The <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Boer</a> Wars had largely been paid for out of taxation; but Lloyd George raised <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_national_debt" title="United Kingdom national debt">debt financing</a> of £321 million. Large (but deferred) increases in Supertax and income tax rates were accompanied by increases in excise duties, and the budget produced a tax increase of £63 million in a full year.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> His last budget, on 4 May 1915, showed a growing concern for the effects of alcohol on the war effort, with large increases in duties, and a scheme of state control of alcohol sales in specified areas. The excise proposals were opposed by the Irish Nationalists and the Conservatives, and were abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_of_Munitions">Minister of Munitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Minister of Munitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg/220px-David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg/330px-David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg/440px-David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1870" data-file-height="2563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Lloyd_George_1915.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George in 1915</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Munitions" title="Minister of Munitions">Minister of Munitions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">Shell Crisis of 1915</a></div>
<p>Lloyd George gained a heroic reputation with his energetic work as Minister of Munitions, 1915–16, setting the stage for his move up to the height of power. After a long struggle with the War Office, he wrested responsibility for arms production away from the generals, making it a purely industrial department, with considerable expert assistance from <a href="/wiki/Walter_Runciman,_1st_Viscount_Runciman_of_Doxford" title="Walter Runciman, 1st Viscount Runciman of Doxford">Walter Runciman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Adams_1975_232–244_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams_1975_232–244-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The two men gained the respect of Liberal cabinet colleagues for improving administrative capabilities, and increasing outputs.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">Shell Crisis of 1915</a> dismayed public opinion with the news that the Army was running short of artillery shells, demands rose for a strong leader to take charge of munitions. In the <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">first coalition ministry</a>, formed in May 1915, Lloyd George was made <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Munitions" title="Minister of Munitions">Minister of Munitions</a>, heading a new department.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In this position he won great acclaim, which formed the basis for his political ascent.  All historians agree that he boosted national morale and focussed attention on the urgent need for greater output, but many also say the increase in munitions output in 1915–16 was due largely to reforms already underway, though not yet effective, before he had even arrived. The Ministry broke through the cumbersome bureaucracy of the War Office, resolved labour problems, rationalised the supply system and dramatically increased production. Within a year it became the largest buyer, seller, and employer in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Adams_1975_232–244_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams_1975_232–244-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M_124_Kitchener_Llyod_Paul_Mantoux_George_Bertie_of_Thame_Asquith_Beyens_de_Broqueville_Bourgeois_Roques_Briand_Lacaze_JOffre_de_Castelnau_Rachitch_Yovanovitch_Vesnitch_Pachitch_Isvolski.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/M_124_Kitchener_Llyod_Paul_Mantoux_George_Bertie_of_Thame_Asquith_Beyens_de_Broqueville_Bourgeois_Roques_Briand_Lacaze_JOffre_de_Castelnau_Rachitch_Yovanovitch_Vesnitch_Pachitch_Isvolski.JPG/280px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/M_124_Kitchener_Llyod_Paul_Mantoux_George_Bertie_of_Thame_Asquith_Beyens_de_Broqueville_Bourgeois_Roques_Briand_Lacaze_JOffre_de_Castelnau_Rachitch_Yovanovitch_Vesnitch_Pachitch_Isvolski.JPG/420px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/M_124_Kitchener_Llyod_Paul_Mantoux_George_Bertie_of_Thame_Asquith_Beyens_de_Broqueville_Bourgeois_Roques_Briand_Lacaze_JOffre_de_Castelnau_Rachitch_Yovanovitch_Vesnitch_Pachitch_Isvolski.JPG/560px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4399" data-file-height="2453" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M_124_Kitchener_Llyod_Paul_Mantoux_George_Bertie_of_Thame_Asquith_Beyens_de_Broqueville_Bourgeois_Roques_Briand_Lacaze_JOffre_de_Castelnau_Rachitch_Yovanovitch_Vesnitch_Pachitch_Isvolski.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lloyd George, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Edward Grey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Herbert Kitchener</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Pa%C5%A1i%C4%87" title="Nikola Pašić">Nikola Pašić</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Salandra" title="Antonio Salandra">Antonio Salandra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Izvolsky" title="Alexander Izvolsky">Alexander Izvolsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Briand" title="Aristide Briand">Aristide Briand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joffre" title="Joseph Joffre">Joseph Joffre</a> at a conference of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied Powers</a> on 27–28 March 1916 in Paris</div></div></div>
<p>Lloyd George was not at all satisfied with the progress of the war. He wanted to "knock away the props", by attacking Germany's allies – from early in 1915 he argued for the sending of British troops to the Balkans to assist Serbia and bring Greece and other Balkan countries onto the side of the Allies (this was eventually done – the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_front" title="Macedonian front">Salonika expedition</a> – although not on the scale that Lloyd George had wanted, and mountain ranges made his suggestions of grand Balkan offensives impractical); in 1916 he wanted to send machine guns to <a href="/wiki/Romania_during_World_War_I" title="Romania during World War I">Romania</a> (insufficient amounts were available for this to be feasible). These suggestions began a period of poor relations with the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Imperial_General_Staff" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief of the Imperial General Staff">Chief of the Imperial General Staff</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(British_Army_officer)" class="mw-redirect" title="William Robertson (British Army officer)">General Robertson</a>, who was "brusque to the point of rudeness" and "barely concealed his contempt for Lloyd George's military opinions", to which he was in the habit of retorting "I've 'eard different".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George persuaded <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Kitchener</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_War" title="Secretary of State for War">Secretary of State for War</a>, to raise a <a href="/wiki/38th_(Welsh)_Infantry_Division" title="38th (Welsh) Infantry Division">Welsh Division</a>, and, despite Kitchener's threat of resignation, to recognise nonconformist chaplains in the Army.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Late in 1915 Lloyd George became a strong supporter of general conscription, an issue that divided Liberals, and helped the passage of several <a href="/wiki/Military_Service_Act_1916" title="Military Service Act 1916">conscription acts</a> from January 1916 onwards. In spring 1916 <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Alfred Milner</a> hoped Lloyd George could be persuaded to bring down the coalition government by resigning, but this did not happen.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State_for_War">Secretary of State for War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Secretary of State for War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg/220px-PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg/330px-PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg/440px-PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="766" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PMLloydGeorge--nsillustratedwar03londuoft.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lloyd George in 1916</div></div></div>
<p>In June 1916 Lloyd George succeeded <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Lord Kitchener</a> (who died when his ship was sunk) as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_War" title="Secretary of State for War">Secretary of State for War</a>, although he had little control over strategy, as General Robertson had been given direct right of access to the Cabinet so as to bypass Kitchener. He did succeed in securing the appointment of Sir <a href="/wiki/Eric_Geddes" title="Eric Geddes">Eric Geddes</a> to take charge of military railways behind British lines in France, with the honorary rank of major-general.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
Lloyd George told a journalist, <a href="/wiki/Roy_W._Howard" title="Roy W. Howard">Roy W. Howard</a>, in late September that "the fight must be to a finish – to a knockout", a rejection of President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>'s offer to mediate.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George was increasingly frustrated at the limited gains of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme_(1916)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of the Somme (1916)">Somme Offensive</a>, criticising <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Haig,_1st_Earl_Haig" title="Douglas Haig, 1st Earl Haig">General Haig</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Foch" title="Ferdinand Foch">Ferdinand Foch</a> on a visit to the Western Front in September (British casualty ratios were worse than those of the French, who were more experienced and had more artillery), proposing sending Robertson on a mission to Russia (he refused to go), and demanding that more troops be sent to Salonika to help Romania. Robertson eventually threatened to resign.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the press still argued that the professional leadership of Haig and Robertson was preferable to civilian interference which had led to disasters like <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kut" title="Siege of Kut">Kut</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Northcliffe" title="Alfred Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Northcliffe">Lord Northcliffe</a>, owner of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> stormed into Lloyd George's office and, finding him unavailable, told his secretary "You can tell him that I hear he has been interfering with Strategy, and that if he goes on I will break him", and the same day (11 October) Lloyd George also received a warning letter from <a href="/wiki/Howell_Arthur_Gwynne" class="mw-redirect" title="Howell Arthur Gwynne">H. A. Gwynne</a>, editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Morning Post">Morning Post</a></i>. He was obliged to give his "word of honour" to Asquith that he had complete confidence in Haig and Robertson and thought them irreplaceable, but he wrote to Robertson wanting to know how their differences had been leaked to the press (affecting to believe that Robertson had not personally "authorised such a breach of confidence &amp; discipline"). He asserted his right to express his opinions about strategy in November, by which time ministers had taken to holding meetings to which Robertson was not invited.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The weakness of Asquith as a planner and organiser was increasingly apparent to senior officials. After Asquith had refused, then agreed, and then refused again to agree to Lloyd George's demand that he should be allowed to chair a small committee to manage the war, he resigned in December 1916. Grey was among leading Asquithians who had identified Lloyd George's intentions the previous month.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Lloyd George became Prime Minister, with the nation demanding he take vigorous charge of the war. A <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> cartoon of the time showed him as "The New Conductor" conducting the orchestra in the "Opening of the 1917 Overture".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although during the political crisis Robertson had advised Lloyd George to "stick to it" and form a small War Council, Lloyd George had planned if necessary to appeal to the country, his Military Secretary Colonel <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lee,_1st_Viscount_Lee_of_Fareham" title="Arthur Lee, 1st Viscount Lee of Fareham">Arthur Lee</a> having prepared a memo blaming Robertson and the General Staff for the loss of Serbia and Romania. Lloyd George was restricted by his promise to the Unionists to keep Haig as Commander-in-Chief and the press support for the generals, although <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner" class="mw-redirect" title="Alfred Milner">Milner</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Nathaniel_Curzon" class="mw-redirect" title="George Nathaniel Curzon">Curzon</a> were also sympathetic to campaigns to increase British power in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> After Germany's offer (12 December 1916) of a negotiated peace Lloyd George rebuffed President Wilson's request for the belligerents to state their war aims by demanding terms tantamount to German defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Prime_Minister_.281916.E2.80.931922.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister_(1916–1922)">Prime Minister (1916–1922)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Prime Minister (1916–1922)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_ministry" title="Lloyd George ministry">Lloyd George ministry</a></div>
<h3><span id="War_leader_.281916.E2.80.931918.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_leader_(1916–1918)">War leader (1916–1918)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: War leader (1916–1918)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Forming_a_government">Forming a government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Forming a government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The fall of Asquith as Prime Minister split the Liberal Party into two factions: those who supported him and those who supported the coalition government. In his <i>War Memoirs</i>, Lloyd George compared himself with Asquith:<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>There are certain indispensable qualities essential to the Chief Minister of the Crown in a great war. . . . Such a minister must have courage, composure, and judgment. All this Mr. Asquith possessed in a superlative degree. . . . But a war minister must also have vision, imagination and initiative—he must show untiring assiduity, must exercise constant oversight and supervision of every sphere of war activity, must possess driving force to energize this activity, must be in continuous consultation with experts, official and unofficial, as to the best means of utilising the resources of the country in conjunction with the Allies for the achievement of victory. If to this can be added a flair for conducting a great fight, then you have an ideal War Minister.</p></blockquote>
<p>After December 1916 Lloyd George relied on the support of Conservatives and of the press baron <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Northcliffe" title="Alfred Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Northcliffe">Lord Northcliffe</a> (who owned both <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i>). Besides the Prime Minister, the five-member <a href="/wiki/War_Cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="War Cabinet">War Cabinet</a> contained three Conservatives (Lord President of the Council and Leader of the House of Lords <a href="/wiki/George_Curzon,_1st_Marquess_Curzon_of_Kedleston" title="George Curzon, 1st Marquess Curzon of Kedleston">Lord Curzon</a>, Chancellor of the Exchequer and Leader of the House of Commons <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, and Minister without Portfolio <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Lord Milner</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Henderson" title="Arthur Henderson">Arthur Henderson</a>, unofficially representing <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Edward Carson</a> was appointed <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Admiralty" title="First Lord of the Admiralty">First Lord of the Admiralty</a>, as had been widely touted during the intrigues of the previous month, but excluded from the War Cabinet. Amongst the few Liberal frontbenchers to support Lloyd George were <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Addison,_1st_Viscount_Addison" title="Christopher Addison, 1st Viscount Addison">Christopher Addison</a> (who had played an important role in drumming up some backbench Liberal support for Lloyd George), <a href="/wiki/H._A._L._Fisher" title="H. A. L. Fisher">H. A. L. Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Alfred_Thomas,_1st_Viscount_Rhondda" class="mw-redirect" title="David Alfred Thomas, 1st Viscount Rhondda">Lord Rhondda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Stanley,_1st_Baron_Ashfield" title="Albert Stanley, 1st Baron Ashfield">Sir Albert Stanley</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Montagu" title="Edwin Montagu">Edwin Montagu</a> and Churchill joined the government in the summer of 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoss1985224_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoss1985224-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lloyd George wanted to make the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> a major British war aim, and two days after taking office told Robertson that he wanted a major victory, preferably the capture of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, to impress British public opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the Rome Conference (5–6 January 1917) Lloyd George was discreetly quiet about plans to take Jerusalem, an object which advanced British interests rather than doing much to win the war. Lloyd George proposed sending heavy guns to Italy with a view to defeating Austria-Hungary, possibly to be balanced by a transfer of Italian troops to Salonika, but was unable to obtain the support of the French or Italians, and Robertson talked of resigning.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nivelle_Affair">Nivelle Affair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Lloyd_George&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nivelle Affair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Lloyd George engaged almost constantly in intrigues calculated to reduce the power of the generals, including trying to subordinate British forces in France to the French <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nivelle" title="Robert Nivelle">General Nivelle</a>. He backed Nivelle because he thought he had 'proved himself to be a Man' by his successful counterattacks at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Verdun</a>, and because of his promises that he could break the German lines in 48 hours. Nivelle increasingly complained of Haig's dragging his feet rather than co-operating with their plans for the offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The plan was to put British forces under Nivelle's direct command for the great 1917 offensive. The British would attack first, thereby tying down the German reserves. Then the French would strike and score an overwhelming victory in two days. It was announced at a War Cabinet meeting on 24 February, to which neither Robertson nor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stanley,_17th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Stanley, 17th Earl of Derby">Lord Derby</a> (Secretary of State for War) had been invited.  Ministers felt that the French generals and staff had shown themselves more skillful than the British in 1916, whilst politically Britain had to give wholehearted support to what would probably be the last major French effort of the war. The Nivelle proposal was then given to Robertson and Haig without warning on 26–27 February (minutes from the War Cabinet meeting were not sent to <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">the King</a> until 28 February, so that he did not have a prior chance to object). Robertson in particular protested vehemently. Finally a compromise was reached whereby Haig would be under Nivelle's orders but would retain operational control of British forces and keep a right of appeal to London "if he saw good reason". After further argument the <i>status quo</i>, that Haig was an ally of the French but was expected to defer to their wishes, was largely restored in mid-March.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the event the British attack at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arras_(1917)" title="Battle of Arras (1917)">Battle of Arras</a> (9–14 April 1917) was partly successful but with much higher casualties than the Germans suffered. There had been many delays and the Germans, suspecting an attack, had shortened their lines to the strong <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_Line" title="Hindenburg Line">Hindenburg Line</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nivelle_Offensive" title="Nivelle Offensive">French attack on the Aisne River in mid-April</a> gained some tactically important high ground but failed to achieve the promised decisive breakthrough, pushing the French Army to the point of <a href="/wiki/French_Army_Mutinies" class="mw-redirect" title="French Army Mutinies">mutiny</a>.
